 So we went back to MIT,,we took out the Instron machine,and we figured outnwhat sort of forces you needed,in order to compressna briquette to the level,that you actually are gettingnimproved performance out of it?,And at the same time that we hadnstudents in the lab looking at this,,we also had community partners in Haitinworking to develop the process,,to improve it and make it more accessiblento people in the villages there.,And after some time,,we developed a low-cost pressnthat allows you to produce charcoal,,which actually now burns not only --,actually, it burns longer,ncleaner than wood charcoal. 
 So now we're in a situationnwhere we have a product,,which is actually better than whatnyou can buy in Haiti in the marketplace,,which is a very wonderful place to be.,In Haiti alone, about 30 million treesnare cut down every year.,There's a possibilitynof this being implemented,and saving a good portion of those.,In addition, the revenue generatednfrom that charcoal is 260 million dollars.,That's an awful lotnfor a country like Haiti --,with a population of eight million,and an average incomenof less than 400 dollars.,So this is where we're also moving aheadnwith our charcoal project.

 Let's take a snapshot at aviation.,And there was a wonderful little short four-year time period,when marvelous things happened.,It started in 1908, when the Wright brothers flew in Paris, and everybody said,,"Ooh, hey, I can do that."  (Applause)I'd like to talk todaynabout the two biggest social trends,in the coming century,,and perhaps in the next 10,000 years.,But I want to startnwith my work on romantic love,,because that's my most recent work.,What I and my colleagues did was putn32 people, who were madly in love,,into a functional MRI brain scanner.,17 who were madly in lovenand their love was accepted;,and 15 who were madly in lovenand they had just been dumped.,And so I want to tell younabout that first,,and then go on into wherenI think love is going. 
 (Laughter) 
 "What 'tis to love?" Shakespeare said.,I think our ancestors --,I think human beings have beennwondering about this question,since they sat around their campfires,or lay and watchednthe stars a million years ago.,I started out by trying to figure outnwhat romantic love was,by looking at the last 45 yearsnof the psychological research,and as it turns out,,there's a very specific group of thingsnthat happen when you fall in love.,The first thing that happens is,,a person begins to take onnwhat I call, "special meaning.",As a truck driver once said to me,,"The world had a new center,nand that center was Mary Anne." 
 George Bernard Shaw said it differently.,"Love consists of overestimatingnthe differences,between one woman and another.",And indeed, that's what we do. 
 (Laughter) 
 And then you just focus on this person.,You can list what youndon't like about them,,but then you sweep that asidenand focus on what you do.,As Chaucer said, "Love is blind." 
 In trying to understand romantic love,,I decided I would read poetrynfrom all over the world,,and I just want to givenyou one very short poem,from eighth-century China,,because it's an almost perfect example,of a man who is focused totallynon a particular woman.,It's a little bit like when you arenmadly in love with somebody,and you walk into a parking lot --,their car is differentnfrom every other car in the parking lot.,Their wine glass at dinner,is different from every other wine glassnat the dinner party.,And in this case, a man got hookednon a bamboo sleeping mat. 
 And it goes like this.,It's by a guy called Yuan Zhen.,"I cannot bear to put awaynthe bamboo sleeping mat.,The night I brought you home,nI watched you roll it out.",He became hooked on a sleeping mat,,probably because of elevated activitynof dopamine in his brain,,just like with you and me. 
 But anyway, not only does this personntake on special meaning,,you focus your attention on them.,You aggrandize them.,But you have intense energy.,As one Polynesian said,n"I felt like jumping in the sky.",You're up all night.nYou're walking till dawn.,You feel intense elationnwhen things are going well;,mood swings into horrible despairnwhen things are going poorly.,Real dependence on this person.,As one businessman in New York said to me,,"Anything she liked, I liked.",Simple. Romantic love is very simple. 
 You become extremely sexually possessive.,You know, if you're just sleepingnwith somebody casually,,you don't really carenif they're sleeping with somebody else.,But the moment you fall in love,,you become extremelynsexually possessive of them.,I think there's a Darwiniannpurpose to this.,The whole point of thisnis to pull two people together,strongly enough to beginnto rear babies as a team. 
 But the main characteristicsnof romantic love are craving:,an intense cravingnto be with a particular person,,not just sexually, but emotionally.,It would be nice to go to bed with them,,but you want them to call younon the telephone, to invite you out, etc.,,to tell you that they love you.,The other main characteristicnis motivation.,The motor in the brain begins to crank,nand you want this person. 
 And last but not least,nit is an obsession.,Before I put these peoplenin the MRI machine,,I would ask them all kinds of questions.,But my most important questionnwas always the same.,It was: "What percentage of the daynand night do you think about this person?",And indeed, they would say,,"All day. All night.,I can never stop thinkingnabout him or her." 
 And then, the very last question --,I would always have to worknmyself up to this question,,because I'm not a psychologist.,I don't work with peoplenin any kind of traumatic situation.,My final question was always the same.,I would say,n"Would you die for him or her?",And, indeed, these people would say "Yes!",as if I had asked them to pass the salt.,I was just staggered by it. 
 So we scanned their brains,,looking at a photographnof their sweetheart,and looking at a neutral photograph,,with a distraction task in between.,So we could look at the same brainnwhen it was in that heightened state,and when it was in a resting state.,And we found activitynin a lot of brain regions.,In fact, one of the most importantnwas a brain region,that becomes activenwhen you feel the rush of cocaine.,And indeed, that's exactly what happens. 
 I began to realize that romantic lovenis not an emotion.,In fact, I had always thoughtnit was a series of emotions,,from very high to very low.,But actually, it's a drive.,It comes from the motor of the mind,,the wanting part of the mind,nthe craving part of the mind.,The kind of part of the mind,when you're reachingnfor that piece of chocolate,,when you want to winnthat promotion at work.,The motor of the brain.,It's a drive. 
 And in fact, I think it's more powerfulnthan the sex drive.,You know, if you ask somebodynto go to bed with you,,and they say, "No, thank you,",you certainly don't kill yourselfnor slip into a clinical depression.,But certainly, around the world,,people who are rejectednin love will kill for it.,People live for love.,They kill for love.,They die for love.,They have songs, poems, novels,,sculptures, paintings, myths, legends.,In over 175 societies,,people have left their evidencenof this powerful brain system.,I have come to think,it's one of the most powerfulnbrain systems on Earth,for both great joy and great sorrow. 
 And I've also come to think,that it's one of threenbasically different brain systems,that evolved from mating and reproduction.,One is the sex drive:nthe craving for sexual gratification.,W.H. Auden called itnan "intolerable neural itch,",and indeed, that's what it is.,It keeps bothering youna little bit, like being hungry.,The second of these three brainnsystems is romantic love:,that elation, obsession of early love.,And the third brain system is attachment:,that sense of calm and securitynyou can feel for a long-term partner. 
 And I think that the sex drivenevolved to get you out there,,looking for a whole range of partners.,You can feel it when you're justndriving along in your car.,It can be focused on nobody.,I think romantic love evolvednto enable you to focus your mating energy,on just one individual at a time,,thereby conserving mating time and energy.,And I think that attachment,nthe third brain system,,evolved to enable you to toleratenthis human being,at least long enough to raisena child together as a team.,So with that preamble,,I want to go into discussingnthe two most profound social trends.,One of the last 10,000 years,and the other,ncertainly of the last 25 years,,that are going to have an impactnon these three different brain systems:,lust, romantic lovenand deep attachment to a partner. 
 The first is women working,nmoving into the workforce.,I've looked at 130 societies,through the demographic yearbooksnof the United Nations.,Everywhere in the world,n129 out of 130 of them,,women are not only movingninto the job market --,sometimes very, very slowly,nbut they are moving into the job market --,and they are very slowly closingnthat gap between men and women,in terms of economic power,nhealth and education.,It's very slow.

 So maybe me starting to takenpictures was some kind of way,to bring both sidesnof my family together --,a way to take the worlds with me,na way to tell stories visually.,It all kind of started that way,,but I think that I really realizednthe power of the image,when I first wentnto the garbage-collecting village,in Egypt, when I was about 16.,My mother took me there.,She's somebody who believes stronglynin community service,,and decided that this was somethingnthat I needed to do.,And so I went there and I metnsome amazing women there.,There was a center there,,where they were teachingnpeople how to read and write,,and get vaccinationsnagainst the many diseases,you can get from sorting through garbage.,And I began teaching there.,I taught English, and I metnsome incredible women there.,I met people that livenseven people to a room,,barely can afford their evening meal,,yet lived with this strengthnof spirit and sense of humor,and just incredible qualities. 
 I got drawn into this communitynand I began to take pictures there.,I took pictures of weddingsnand older family members --,things that they wanted memories of.,About two years after I startedntaking these pictures,,the UN Conferencenon Population and Development,asked me to show them at the conference.,So I was 18; I was very excited.,It was my first exhibit of photographsnand they were all put up there,,and after about two days,nthey all came down except for three.,People were very upset,,very angry that I was showingnthese dirty sides of Cairo,,and why didn't I cutnthe dead donkey out of the frame?,And as I sat there, I got very depressed.,I looked at this big empty wallnwith three lonely photographs,that were, you know,nvery pretty photographs,and I was like, "I failed at this.",But I was looking at this intensenemotion and intense feeling,that had come out of peoplenjust seeing these photographs.,Here I was, this 18-year-old pipsqueaknthat nobody listened to,,and all of a sudden,nI put these photographs on the wall,,and there were arguments,nand they had to be taken down.,And I saw the power of the image,nand it was incredible.,And I think the most important reactionnthat I saw there was actually from people,that would never have gonento the garbage village themselves,,that would never have seennthat the human spirit could thrive,in such difficult circumstances.,And I think it was at that point,that I decided I wanted to usenphotography and film,to somehow bridge gaps,nto bridge cultures,,bring people together, cross borders.,And so that's what reallynkind of started me off. 
 Did a stint at MTV, made a filmncalled "Startup.com,",and I've done a couple of music films.,But in 2003, when the warnin Iraq was about to start,,it was a very surreal feeling for me,,because before the war started,,there was kind of this media warnthat was going on.,And I was watching television in New York,,and there seemed to be just onenpoint of view that was coming across,,and the coverage wentnfrom the US State Department,to embedded troops.,And what was coming across on the news,was that there was going to be thisnclean war and precision bombings,,and the Iraqis would be greetingnthe Americans as liberators,,and throwing flowers at their feetnin the streets of Baghdad.,And I knew that therenwas a completely other story,that was taking place in the Middle East,nwhere my parents were.,I knew that there wasna completely other story being told,,and I was thinking, "How are peoplensupposed to communicate with each other,when they're getting completelyndifferent messages,,and nobody knowsnwhat the other's being told?,How are people supposed to havenany kind of common understanding,or know how to move togetherninto the future?,So I knew that I had to go there.,I just wanted to be in the center.,I had no plan.  Chris Anderson: Jill Sobule!If you're here today --,and I'm very happy that you are --,you've all heard aboutnhow sustainable development,will save us from ourselves.,However, when we're not at TED,nwe are often told,that a real sustainability policy agendanis just not feasible,,especially in large urban areasnlike New York City.,And that's because most peoplenwith decision-making powers,,in both the public and the private sector,,really don't feelnas though they're in danger. 
 The reason why I'm here today,nin part, is because of a dog --,an abandoned puppy I foundnback in the rain, back in 1998.,She turned out to bena much bigger dog than I'd anticipated.,When she came into my life, we werenfighting against a huge waste facility,planned for the East River waterfront,despite the fact thatnour small part of New York City,already handled more than 40 percentnof the entire city's commercial waste:,a sewage treatment pelletizing plant,na sewage sludge plant, four power plants,,the world's largestnfood-distribution center,,as well as other industries that bringnmore than 60,000 diesel truck trips,to the area each week.,The area also has one of the lowest ratiosnof parks to people in the city. 
 So when I was contactednby the Parks Department,about a $10,000 seed-grant initiativento help develop waterfront projects,,I thought they were reallynwell-meaning, but a bit naive.,I'd lived in this area all my life,nand you could not get to the river,,because of all the lovely facilitiesnthat I mentioned earlier.,Then, while joggingnwith my dog one morning,,she pulled me into what I thoughtnwas just another illegal dump.,There were weeds and piles of garbagenand other stuff that I won't mention here,,but she kept dragging me,,and lo and behold, at the endnof that lot was the river.,I knew that this forgottennlittle street-end,,abandoned like the dog that broughtnme there, was worth saving.,And I knew it would grownto become the proud beginnings,of the community-led revitalizationnof the new South Bronx. 
 And just like my new dog, it was an ideanthat got bigger than I'd imagined.,We garnered much support along the way,,and the Hunts Point Riverside Parknbecame the first waterfront park,that the South Bronx had hadnin more than 60 years.,We leveraged that $10,000 seed grantnmore than 300 times,,into a $3 million park. 
 And in the fall, I'm going to exchangenmarriage vows with my beloved.

 Thank you very much.

 That's him pressing my buttonsnback there, which he does all the time. 
 (Laughter) 
 (Applause) 
 But those of us livingnin environmental justice communities,are the canary in the coal mine.,We feel the problems right now,nand have for some time.,Environmental justice, for those of younwho may not be familiar with the term,,goes something like this:,no community should be saddlednwith more environmental burdens,and less environmental benefitsnthan any other. 
 Unfortunately, race and classnare extremely reliable indicators,as to where one might find the good stuff,nlike parks and trees,,and where one might find the bad stuff,nlike power plants and waste facilities.,As a black person in America,nI am twice as likely as a white person,to live in an area where air pollutionnposes the greatest risk to my health.,I am five times more likelynto live within walking distance,of a power plant or chemical facility,,which I do.,These land-use decisionsncreated the hostile conditions,that lead to problems like obesity,ndiabetes and asthma.,Why would someone leave their home to gonfor a brisk walk in a toxic neighborhood?,Our 27 percent obesity ratenis high even for this country,,and diabetes comes with it.,One out of four South Bronxnchildren has asthma.,Our asthma hospitalization rate,is seven times highernthan the national average.,These impacts are coming everyone's way.,And we all pay dearlynfor solid waste costs,,health problems associatednwith pollution and more odiously,,the cost of imprisoningnour young black and Latino men,,who possess untold amountsnof untapped potential.,Fifty percent of our residentsnlive at or below the poverty line;,25 percent of us are unemployed.,Low-income citizens often usenemergency-room visits as primary care.,This comes at a high cost to taxpayersnand produces no proportional benefits.,Poor people are not only still poor,nthey are still unhealthy. 
 Fortunately, there are many peoplenlike me who are striving for solutions,that won't compromise the lives,of low-income communities of colornin the short term,,and won't destroy us all in the long term.,None of us want that,nand we all have that in common.,So what else do we have in common? 
 Well, first of all,nwe're all incredibly good-looking. 
 (Laughter) 
 Graduated high school, college,npost-graduate degrees,,traveled to interesting places,ndidn't have kids in your early teens,,financially stable, never been imprisoned.,OK. Good. 
 (Laughter) 
 But, besides being a black woman,,I am different from most of younin some other ways.,I watched nearly half of the buildingsnin my neighborhood burn down.,My big brother Lenny fought in Vietnam,,only to be gunned downna few blocks from our home.,Jesus.,I grew up with a crack housenacross the street.,Yeah, I'm a poor black childnfrom the ghetto.,These things make me different from you.,But the things we have in common,set me apart from mostnof the people in my community,,and I am in between these two worlds,with enough of my heartnto fight for justice in the other. 
 So how did things get so different for us?,In the late '40s, my dad --na Pullman porter, son of a slave --,bought a house in the Hunts Pointnsection of the South Bronx,,and a few years later, he married my mom.,At the time, the community was a mostlynwhite, working-class neighborhood.,My dad was not alone.,And as others like him pursuedntheir own version of the American dream,,white flight became commonnin the South Bronx,and in many cities around the country.,Red-lining was used by banks,nwherein certain sections of the city,,including ours, were deemednoff-limits to any sort of investment.,Many landlords believed it was morenprofitable to torch their buildings,and collect insurance money rathernthan to sell under those conditions --,dead or injured former tenantsnnotwithstanding. 
 Hunts Point was formerlyna walk-to-work community,,but now residents had neithernwork nor home to walk to.,A national highway construction boomnwas added to our problems.,In New York State,,Robert Moses spearheadednan aggressive highway-expansion campaign.,One of its primary goals wasnto make it easier,for residents of wealthy communitiesnin Westchester County to go to Manhattan.,The South Bronx, which lies in between,ndid not stand a chance.,Residents were often givennless than a month's notice,before their buildings were razed.,600,000 people were displaced.,The common perception was,that only pimps and pushersnand prostitutes were from the South Bronx.,And if you are toldnfrom your earliest days,that nothing good is going to comenfrom your community,,that it's bad and ugly,,how could it not reflect on you?,So now, my family's propertynwas worthless,,save for that it was our home,nand all we had.,And luckily for me, that homenand the love inside of it,,along with help from teachers, mentorsnand friends along the way, was enough. 
 Now, why is this story important?,Because from a planning perspective,,economic degradationnbegets environmental degradation,,which begets social degradation.,The disinvestment that begannin the 1960s set the stage,for all the environmentalninjustices that were to come.,Antiquated zoning and land-usenregulations are still used to this day,to continue putting pollutingnfacilities in my neighborhood.,Are these factors taken into considerationnwhen land-use policy is decided?,What costs are associatednwith these decisions?,And who pays? Who profits?,Does anything justifynwhat the local community goes through?,This was "planning" -- in quotes --,that did not havenour best interests in mind. 
 Once we realized that, we decidednit was time to do our own planning.,That small park I told you about earlier,was the first stage of buildingna Greenway movement in the South Bronx.,I wrote a one-and-a-quarter-millionndollar federal transportation grant,to design the plannfor a waterfront esplanade,with dedicated on-street bike paths.,Physical improvements help informnpublic policy regarding traffic safety,,the placement of the wastenand other facilities,,which, if done properly, don't compromisena community's quality of life.,They provide opportunitiesnto be more physically active,,as well as local economic development.,Think bike shops, juice stands.,We secured 20 million dollarsnto build first-phase projects.,This is Lafayette Avenue --,and that's redesignednby Mathews Nielsen Landscape Architects.,And once this path is constructed,nit'll connect the South Bronx,with more than 400 acresnof Randall's Island Park.,Right now we're separated by about 25 feetnof water, but this link will change that. 
 As we nurture the natural environment,nits abundance will give us back even more.,We run a project called the Bronxn[Environmental] Stewardship Training,,which provides job training in the fieldsnof ecological restoration,,so that folks from our communitynhave the skills to compete,for these well-paying jobs.,Little by little, we're seedingnthe area with green-collar jobs --,and with people that have bothna financial and personal stake,in their environment.,The Sheridan Expresswaynis an underutilized relic,of the Robert Moses era,,built with no regard for the neighborhoodsnthat were divided by it.,Even during rush hour,nit goes virtually unused.,The community creatednan alternative transportation plan,that allows for the removalnof the highway.,We have the opportunity now to bringntogether all the stakeholders,to re-envision how this 28 acresncan be better utilized,for parkland, affordable housingnand local economic development. 
 We also built New York City's first greennand cool roof demonstration project,on top of our offices.,Cool roofs are highly-reflectivensurfaces that don't absorb solar heat,,and pass it on tonthe building or atmosphere.,Green roofs are soil and living plants.,Both can be used insteadnof petroleum-based roofing materials,that absorb heat, contributento urban "heat island" effect,and degrade under the sun,,which we in turn breathe.,Green roofs also retainnup to 75 percent of rainfall,,so they reduce a city's need to fundncostly end-of-pipe solutions --,which, incidentally, are often located,in environmental justicencommunities like mine.,And they provide habitatsnfor our little friends!

 Prior to Katrina, the South Bronxnand New Orleans' Ninth Ward,had a lot in common.,Both were largely populatednby poor people of color,,both hotbeds of cultural innovation:nthink hip-hop and jazz.,Both are waterfront communitiesnthat host both industries and residents,in close proximity of one another.,In the post-Katrina era,nwe have still more in common.,We're at best ignored,nand maligned and abused, at worst,,by negligent regulatory agencies,npernicious zoning,and lax governmental accountability.,Neither the destruction of the Ninth Wardnnor the South Bronx was inevitable.,But we have emerged with valuable lessons,about how to dig ourselves out.,We are more than simplynnational symbols of urban blight,or problems to be solvednby empty campaign promises,of presidents come and gone.,Now will we let the Gulf Coastnlanguish for a decade or two,,like the South Bronx did?,Or will we take proactive steps,and learn from the homegrown resourcenof grassroots activists,that have been born of desperationnin communities like mine?

 But -- I know it's the bottom line,nor one's perception of it,,that motivates people in the end.,I'm interested in what I like to callnthe "triple bottom line",that sustainable development can produce.,Developments that have the potentialnto create positive returns,for all concerned:nthe developers, government,and the communitynwhere these projects go up. 
 At present, that's not happeningnin New York City.,And we are operating with a comprehensivenurban-planning deficit.,A parade of government subsidies,is going to propose big-box and stadiumndevelopments in the South Bronx,,but there is scant coordinationnbetween city agencies,on how to deal with the cumulative effectsnof increased traffic, pollution,,solid waste and the impacts on open space.,And their approaches to local economicnand job development are so lame,it's not even funny.,Because on top of that,,the world's richest sports teamnis replacing the House That Ruth Built,by destroying twonwell-loved community parks.,Now, we'll have even lessnthan that stat I told you about earlier.,And although less than 25 percentnof South Bronx residents own cars,,these projects includenthousands of new parking spaces,,yet zip in terms of mass public transit.,Now, what's missing from the larger debate,is a comprehensive cost-benefit analysis,between not fixing an unhealthy,nenvironmentally-challenged community,,versus incorporating structural,nsustainable changes.,My agency is working closelynwith Columbia University and others,to shine a light on these issues. 
 Now let's get this straight:nI am not anti-development.,Ours is a city, not a wilderness preserve.,And I've embraced my inner capitalist.,And, but I don't have --

 So I don't have a problemnwith developers making money.,There's enough precedent out therento show that a sustainable,,community-friendly developmentncan still make a fortune.,Fellow TEDsters Bill McDonoughnand Amory Lovins --,both heroes of mine by the way --nhave shown that you can actually do that.,I do have a problemnwith developments that hyper-exploit,politically vulnerablencommunities for profit.,That it continues is a shame upon us all,,because we are all responsiblenfor the future that we create.,But one of the things I donto remind myself of greater possibilities,,is to learn from visionariesnin other cities.,This is my version of globalization. 
 Let's take Bogota.,Poor, Latino, surrounded bynrunaway gun violence and drug trafficking;,a reputation not unlikenthat of the South Bronx.,However, this city was blessednin the late 1990s,with a highly-influentialnmayor named Enrique Pealosa.,He looked at the demographics.,Few Bogotanos own cars,,yet a huge portion of the city's resourcesnwas dedicated to serving them.,If you're a mayor, you canndo something about that.,His administration narrowed key municipalnthoroughfares from five lanes to three,,outlawed parking on those streets,,expanded pedestrian walkwaysnand bike lanes,,created public plazas,,created one of the most efficientnbus mass-transit systems,in the entire world.,For his brilliant efforts,nhe was nearly impeached.,But as people began to seenthat they were being put first,on issues reflectingntheir day-to-day lives,,incredible things happened.,People stopped littering.,Crime rates dropped, because the streetsnwere alive with people.,His administration attacked severalntypical urban problems at one time,,and on a third-world budget, at that.,We have no excusenin this country, I'm sorry.,But the bottom line is:ntheir people-first agenda,was not meant to penalizenthose who could actually afford cars,,but rather, to provide opportunitiesnfor all Bogotanos to participate,in the city's resurgence.,That development should not come,at the expense of the majoritynof the population,is still consideredna radical idea here in the U.S.,But Bogota's examplenhas the power to change that.

It's a bottle whose inside is its outside.,It has zero volume. And it's non-orientable.,It has wonderful properties.,If you take two Mobius loops and sew their common edge together,,you get one of these, and I make them out of glass.,And I'd love to talk to you about this,,but I don't have much in the way of ... things to say because --,(Laughter)

 However, the "D" in TED of course stands for design.,Just two weeks ago I made --,you know, I've been making small, medium and big Klein bottles for the trade.,But what I've just made --,and I'm delighted to show you, first time in public here.,This is a Klein bottle wine bottle,,which, although in four dimensions,it shouldn't be able to hold any fluid at all,,it's perfectly capable of doing so,because our universe has only three spatial dimensions.,And because our universe is only three spatial dimensions,,it can hold fluids.,So it's highly -- that one's the cool one.,That was a month of my life.,But although I would love to talk about topology with you, I'm not going to.,(Laughter) 
 Instead, I'm going to mention my mom,,who passed away last summer.,Had collected photographs of me, as mothers will do.,Could somebody put this guy up?,And I looked over her album,and she had collected a picture of me, standing --,well, sitting -- in 1969, in front of a bunch of dials.,And I looked at it, and said, oh my god,,that was me, when I was working at the electronic music studio!,As a technician, repairing and maintaining,the electronic music studio at SUNY Buffalo.  Soon after that, I found in another picture that she had, a picture of me.,This guy over here of course is me.,This man is Robert Moog,,the inventor of the Moog synthesizer,,who passed away this past August.,Robert Moog was a generous, kind person, extraordinarily competent engineer.,A musician who took time from his life to teach me,,a sophomore, a freshman at SUNY Buffalo.,He'd come up from Trumansburg to teach me,not just about the Moog synthesizer, but we'd be sitting there --,I'm studying physics at the time.  So, sorry -- it just feels like I should be doing stand-up up here because of the setting.,No, what I want to do is take you back to 1854,in London for the next few minutes, and tell the story --,in brief -- of this outbreak,,which in many ways, I think, helped create the world that we live in today,,and particularly the kind of city that we live in today.,This period in 1854, in the middle part of the 19th century,,in London's history, is incredibly interesting for a number of reasons.,But I think the most important one is that,London was this city of 2.5 million people,,and it was the largest city on the face of the planet at that point.,But it was also the largest city that had ever been built. 
 And so the Victorians were trying to live through,and simultaneously invent a whole new scale of living:,this scale of living that we, you know, now call "metropolitan living.",And it was in many ways, at this point in the mid-1850s, a complete disaster.,They were basically a city living with a modern kind of industrial metropolis,with an Elizabethan public infrastructure.,So people, for instance, just to gross you out for a second,,had cesspools of human waste in their basement. Like, a foot to two feet deep.,And they would just kind of throw the buckets down there,and hope that it would somehow go away,,and of course it never really would go away.,And all of this stuff, basically, had accumulated to the point,where the city was incredibly offensive to just walk around in. 
 It was an amazingly smelly city. Not just because of the cesspools,,but also the sheer number of livestock in the city would shock people.,Not just the horses, but people had cows in their attics that they would use for milk,,that they would hoist up there and keep them in the attic,until literally their milk ran out and they died,,and then they would drag them off to the bone boilers down the street.,So, you would just walk around London at this point,and just be overwhelmed with this stench.,And what ended up happening is that an entire emerging public health system,became convinced that it was the smell that was killing everybody,,that was creating these diseases,that would wipe through the city every three or four years.,And cholera was really the great killer of this period. 
 It arrived in London in 1832, and every four or five years,another epidemic would take 10,000, 20,000 people in London,and throughout the U.K.,And so the authorities became convinced that this smell was this problem.,We had to get rid of the smell.,And so, in fact, they concocted a couple of early, you know,,founding public-health interventions in the system of the city,,one of which was called the "Nuisances Act,",which they got everybody as far as they could,to empty out their cesspools and just pour all that waste into the river.,Because if we get it out of the streets, it'll smell much better,,and -- oh right, we drink from the river.,So what ended up happening, actually,,is they ended up increasing the outbreaks of cholera,because, as we now know, cholera is actually in the water.,It's a waterborne disease, not something that's in the air.,It's not something you smell or inhale; it's something you ingest. 
 And so one of the founding moments of public health in the 19th century,effectively poisoned the water supply of London much more effectively,than any modern day bioterrorist could have ever dreamed of doing.,So this was the state of London in 1854,,and in the middle of all this carnage and offensive conditions,,and in the midst of all this scientific confusion,about what was actually killing people,,it was a very talented classic 19th century multi-disciplinarian named John Snow,,who was a local doctor in Soho in London,,who had been arguing for about four or five years,that cholera was, in fact, a waterborne disease,,and had basically convinced nobody of this.,The public health authorities had largely ignored what he had to say.,And he'd made the case in a number of papers and done a number of studies,,but nothing had really stuck.,And part of -- what's so interesting about this story to me,is that in some ways, it's a great case study in how cultural change happens,,how a good idea eventually comes to win out over much worse ideas.,And Snow labored for a long time with this great insight that everybody ignored. 
 And then on one day, August 28th of 1854,,a young child, a five-month-old girl whose first name we don't know,,we know her only as Baby Lewis, somehow contracted cholera,,came down with cholera at 40 Broad Street.,You can't really see it in this map, but this is the map,that becomes the central focus in the second half of my book.,It's in the middle of Soho, in this working class neighborhood,,this little girl becomes sick and it turns out that the cesspool,,that they still continue to have, despite the Nuisances Act,,bordered on an extremely popular water pump,,local watering hole that was well known for the best water in all of Soho,,that all the residents from Soho and the surrounding neighborhoods would go to. 
 And so this little girl inadvertently ended up,contaminating the water in this popular pump,,and one of the most terrifying outbreaks in the history of England,erupted about two or three days later.,Literally, 10 percent of the neighborhood died in seven days,,and much more would have died if people hadn't fled,after the initial outbreak kicked in.,So it was this incredibly terrifying event.,You had these scenes of entire families dying,over the course of 48 hours of cholera,,alone in their one-room apartments, in their little flats.,Just an extraordinary, terrifying scene.,Snow lived near there, heard about the outbreak,,and in this amazing act of courage went directly into the belly of the beast,because he thought an outbreak that concentrated,could actually potentially end up convincing people that,,in fact, the real menace of cholera was in the water supply and not in the air.,He suspected an outbreak that concentrated,would probably involve a single point source.,One single thing that everybody was going to,because it didn't have the traditional slower path,of infections that you might expect. 
 And so he went right in there and started interviewing people.,He eventually enlisted the help of this amazing other figure,,who's kind of the other protagonist of the book --,this guy, Henry Whitehead, who was a local minister,,who was not at all a man of science, but was incredibly socially connected;,he knew everybody in the neighborhood.,And he managed to track down, Whitehead did,,many of the cases of people who had drunk water from the pump,,or who hadn't drunk water from the pump.,And eventually Snow made a map of the outbreak.,He found increasingly that people who drank from the pump were getting sick.,People who hadn't drunk from the pump were not getting sick.,And he thought about representing that,as a kind of a table of statistics of people living in different neighborhoods,,people who hadn't, you know, percentages of people who hadn't,,but eventually he hit upon the idea,that what he needed was something that you could see.,Something that would take in a sense a higher-level view,of all this activity that had been happening in the neighborhood. 
 And so he created this map,,which basically ended up representing all the deaths in the neighborhoods,as black bars at each address.,And you can see in this map, the pump right at the center of it,and you can see that one of the residences down the way,had about 15 people dead.,And the map is actually a little bit bigger.,As you get further and further away from the pump,,the deaths begin to grow less and less frequent.,And so you can see this something poisonous,emanating out of this pump that you could see in a glance.,And so, with the help of this map,,and with the help of more evangelizing,that he did over the next few years,and that Whitehead did, eventually, actually,,the authorities slowly started to come around.,It took much longer than sometimes we like to think in this story,,but by 1866, when the next big cholera outbreak came to London,,the authorities had been convinced -- in part because of this story,,in part because of this map -- that in fact the water was the problem. 
 And they had already started building the sewers in London,,and they immediately went to this outbreak,and they told everybody to start boiling their water.,And that was the last time that London has seen a cholera outbreak since.,So, part of this story, I think -- well, it's a terrifying story,,it's a very dark story and it's a story,that continues on in many of the developing cities of the world.,It's also a story really that is fundamentally optimistic,,which is to say that it's possible to solve these problems,if we listen to reason, if we listen to the kind of wisdom of these kinds of maps,,if we listen to people like Snow and Whitehead,,if we listen to the locals who understand,what's going on in these kinds of situations.,And what it ended up doing is making the idea,of large-scale metropolitan living a sustainable one. 
 When people were looking at 10 percent of their neighborhoods dying,in the space of seven days,,there was a widespread consensus that this couldn't go on,,that people weren't meant to live in cities of 2.5 million people.,But because of what Snow did, because of this map,,because of the whole series of reforms,that happened in the wake of this map,,we now take for granted that cities have 10 million people,,cities like this one are in fact sustainable things.,We don't worry that New York City is going to collapse in on itself,quite the way that, you know, Rome did,,and be 10 percent of its size in 100 years or 200 years.,And so that in a way is the ultimate legacy of this map.,It's a map of deaths that ended up creating a whole new way of life,,the life that we're enjoying here today. Thank you very much.Thank you.,And I feel like this whole evening has been very amazing to me.,I feel it's sort of like the Vimalakirti Sutra,,an ancient work from ancient India,in which the Buddha appears at the beginning and a whole bunch of people,come to see him from the biggest city in the area, Vaishali,,and they bring some sort of jeweled parasols to make an offering to him.,All the young people, actually, from the city.,The old fogeys don't come because they're mad at Buddha,,because when he came to their city he accepted --,he always accepts the first invitation that comes to him, from whoever it is,,and the local geisha, a movie-star sort of person,,raced the elders of the city in a chariot and invited him first.

You never get any satisfaction that way.,So then you decide, "Well, I'm sick of myself.,I'm going to think of how other people can be happy.,I'm going to get up in the morning and think,,what can I do for even one other person, even a dog, my dog, my cat,,my pet, my butterfly?",And the first person who gets happy when you do that,,you don't do anything for anybody else, but you get happier, you yourself,,because your whole perception broadens,and you suddenly see the whole world and all of the people in it.,And you realize that this -- being with these people --,is the flower garden that Chiho showed us.,It is Nirvana.,And my time is up. And I know the TED commandments.,Thank you.I'd like to begin this song I wrote,about ceaseless yearning and never-ending want,with a poem of popular Petrarchan paradoxes,by Sir Thomas Wyatt the Elder:,"I find no peace, and all my war is done;,I fear and hope, I burn and freeze like ice;,I fly above the wind,,and yet I cannot arise;,And naught I have,,and all the world I seize upon."
